<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The purpose-related reasons that argue for a bail-favorable reading are also applicable here.  Congress did not consider the problem of long-term detention.  It wrote the statute with brief detention in mind.  See H.&nbsp;R. Rep. No. 104-469, pt. 1, p. 123, and n. 25 (1996) (stating that the "average stay [was] 28 days").  Congress did not know (for apparently the Government did not know in <em>Demore</em>) that the average length of detention for this class would turn out to be about a year.  Nor did Congress necessarily know that about 40% of class members eventually obtain the right to remain in the United States.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I should add that reading the statute as denying bail to those whose detention is prolonged is anomalous.  Those whose removal is legally or factually questionable could be imprisoned indefinitely while the matter is being decided.  Those whose removal is not questionable (for they are under a final removal order) could be further imprisoned for no more than six months.  See <em>supra</em>,<em> </em>at 4, 17.<em> </em> In fact, even before our decision in <em>Zadvydas</em>, the Government gave bail hearings to noncitizens under a final order of removal after six months of detention.  See 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 683.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER"><em>Other Applicants for Admission</em></p>
</h3><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The statutory provision that governs the third category of noncitizens seeking admission at the border is 1225(b)(2)(A).  It says that "if the examining immigration officer determines that an alien seeking admission is not clearly and beyond a doubt entitled to be admitted, the alien shall be detained for a proceeding under section 1229a of this title."  See Appendix A-3, <em>infra</em>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Government tells us that this miscellaneous category consists of persons who are neither (1) clearly eligible for admission, nor (2) clearly ineligible.  Pet. for Cert. 3-4.  A clearly eligible person is, of course, immediately admitted.  A clearly ineligible person--someone who lacks the required documents, or provides fraudulent ones--is "removed .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. without further hearing or review."  1225(b)(1)(A)(i); see 1182(a)(6)(C), (a)(7).  But where the matter is not clear, <em>i.e.</em>, where the immigration officer determines that an alien "is not clearly and beyond a doubt entitled to be admitted," he is detained for a removal proceeding.  1225(b)(2)(A).  Like all respondents, this class has been detained for at least six months.  It may include persons returning to the United States who have work permits or other documents seemingly entitling them to entry, but whom an immigration officer suspects are inadmissible for some other reason, such as because they may have incomplete vaccinations or have committed student visa abuse or a crime of "moral turpitude."  See 1182(a) (delineating classes of aliens ineligible for admission).  For instance, the Federal Register is replete with examples of offenses that immigration authorities have thought are crimes of moral turpitude but that the courts of appeals later determine are not.  See, <em>e.g., Goldeshtein </em>v. <em>INS</em>, 8 F.&nbsp;3d 645, 648 (CA9 1993) (structuring financial transactions to avoid currency reports); <em>Nunez </em>v. <em>Holder</em>, 594 F.&nbsp;3d 1124, 1138 (CA9 2010) (indecent exposure).  It also may include individuals who claim citizenship by virtue of birth or parentage but who lack documents clearly proving their claim.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The critical statutory words are the same as those I have just discussed in the context of the asylum seekers--"shall be detained."  There is no more plausible reason here than there was there to believe those words foreclose bail.  See <em>supra</em>, at 20-24.  The constitutional considerations, the statutory language, and the purposes underlying the statute are virtually the same.  Thus, the result should be the same: Given the constitutional considerations, we should interpret the statute as permitting bail.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">IV</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority concludes in Part V, <em>ante,</em> at 29-31, by saying that, before considering bail-related constitutional arguments, the lower courts "should reexamine whether respondents can continue litigating their claims as a class."  <em>Ante, </em>at 29.  Relying on dicta in <em>Reno </em>v. <em>American-Arab Anti-Discrimination Comm.,</em> 525 U.&nbsp;S. 471 (1999) (<em>AADC</em>), it then suggests that the respondents may not be able to continue litigating because the Act says that</p>
<blockquote><p>"no court (other than the Supreme Court) shall have jurisdiction or authority to enjoin or restrain the operation [of the statutory provisions here at issue] other than with respect to the application of such provisions to an individual alien against whom proceedings under such part have been initiated."  8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1252(f&nbsp;)(1).</p>
</blockquote><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Were the majority's suggestion correct as to this jurisdictional question, it would have shown, at most, that we should decide the constitutional question here and now.  We have already asked for and received briefs on that question.  But I do not believe the majority is correct.  Every member of the classes before us falls within the provision's exception.  Every one of them is an "individual alien against whom proceedings under such part have been initiated."  <em>Ibid.</em>  The Court in <em>AADC </em>did not consider, and had no reason to consider, the application of 1252(f)(1) to such a class.  Regardless, a court could order declaratory relief.  Federal Rule of Civil Procedure 23(b)(2) permits a class action where "final injunctive relief or <em>corresponding</em> declaratory relief is appropriate respecting the class as a whole."  (Emphasis added.)  And the Advisory Committee says that declaratory relief can fall within the Rule's term "corresponding" if it "serves as a basis for later injunctive relief."  Notes on Rule 23(b)(2)-1966 Amendment, 28 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. App., p. 812.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Jurisdiction also is unaffected by 8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1252(b)(9), which by its terms applies only "[w]ith respect to review of an order of removal under [1252(a)(1)]."  1252(b).  Respondents challenge their detention without bail, not an order of removal.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Neither does <em>Wal-Mart Stores, Inc. </em>v. <em>Dukes</em>, 564 U.&nbsp;S. 338 (2011), bar these class actions.  Every member of each class seeks the same relief (a bail hearing), every member has been denied that relief, and the differences in situation among members of the class are not relevant to their entitlement to a bail hearing.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;At a minimum I can find nothing in the statute or in the cases to which the majority refers that would prevent the respondents from pursuing their action, obtaining a declaratory judgment, and then using that judgment to obtain relief, namely, a bail hearing, in an individual case.  Thus, I believe the lower courts are free to consider the constitutionality of the relevant statutory provisions as the majority now interprets them.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">V</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER"><em>Conclusion</em></p>
</h3><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The relevant constitutional language, purposes, history, traditions, context, and case law, taken together, make it likely that, where confinement of the noncitizens before us is prolonged (presumptively longer than six months), bail proceedings are constitutionally required.  Given this serious constitutional problem, I would interpret the statutory provisions before us as authorizing bail.  Their language permits that reading, it furthers their basic purposes, and it is consistent with the history, tradition, and constitutional values associated with bail proceedings.  I believe that those bail proceedings should take place in accordance with customary rules of procedure and burdens of proof rather than the special rules that the Ninth Circuit imposed.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The bail questions before us are technical but at heart they are simple.  We need only recall the words of the Declaration of Independence, in particular its insistence that <em>all</em> men and women have "certain unalienable Rights," and that among them is the right to "Liberty."  We need merely remember that the Constitution's Due Process Clause protects each person's liberty from arbitrary deprivation.  And we need just keep in mind the fact that, since Blackstone's time and long before, liberty has included the right of a confined person to seek release on bail.  It is neither technical nor unusually difficult to read the words of these statutes as consistent with this basic right.  I would find it far more difficult, indeed, I would find it alarming, to believe that Congress wrote these statutory words in order to put thousands of individuals at risk of lengthy confinement all within the United States but all without hope of bail.  I would read the statutory words as consistent with, indeed as requiring protection of, the basic right to seek bail.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Because the majority does not do so, with respect, I dissent.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">APPENDIXES</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h3><h3><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h3><h3><p align="CENTER"><em>Statute Applicable to Asylum Seekers</em></p>
</h3><p>8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1225.  "Inspection by immigration officers; expedited removal of inadmissible arriving aliens; referral for hearing</p>
<p align="CENTER">.....</p>
<p>"(b) Inspection of applicants for admission</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(1) Inspection of aliens arriving in the United States and certain other aliens who have not been admitted or paroled</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(A) Screening</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(i) In general</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"If an immigration officer determines that an alien (other than an alien described in subparagraph (F)) who is arriving in the United States or is described in clause (iii) is inadmissible under section 1182(a)(6)(C) or 1182(a)(7) of this title, the officer shall order the alien removed from the United States without further hearing or review unless the alien indicates either an intention to apply for asylum under section 1158 of this title or a fear of persecution.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(ii) Claims for asylum</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"If an immigration officer determines that an alien (other than an alien described in subparagraph (F)) who is arriving in the United States or is described in clause (iii) is inadmissible under section 1182(a)(6)(C) or 1182(a)(7) of this title and the alien indicates either an intention to apply for asylum under section 1158 of this title or a fear of persecution, the officer shall refer the alien for an interview by an asylum officer under subparagraph (B).</p>
<p align="CENTER">.....</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(B) Asylum interviews</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(i) Conduct by asylum officers</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"An asylum officer shall conduct interviews of aliens referred under subparagraph (A)(ii), either at a port of entry or at such other place designated by the Attorney General.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(ii) Referral of certain aliens</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"If the officer determines at the time of the interview that an alien has a credible fear of persecution (within the meaning of clause (v)), the<em> </em>alien<em> shall be detained for further consideration of the application for asylum.</em>"  (Emphasis added.)</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER"><em>Statute Applicable to Criminal Aliens</em></p>
</h3><p>8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1226.  "Apprehension and detention of aliens</p>
<p>"(a) Arrest, detention, and release</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"On a warrant issued by the Attorney General, an alien may be arrested and detained pending a decision on whether the alien is to be removed from the United States.  Except as provided in subsection (c) of this section and pending such decision, the Attorney General--</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(1) may continue to detain the arrested alien; and</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(2) may release the alien on--</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(A) <em>bond </em>of at least $1,500 with security approved by, and containing conditions prescribed by, the Attorney General; or</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(B) conditional parole;</p>
<p align="CENTER">.....</p>
<p>"(c) Detention of criminal aliens</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(1) Custody</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The Attorney General <em>shall take into custody</em> any alien who--</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(A) is inadmissible by reason of having committed any offense covered in section 1182(a)(2) of this title,</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(B) is deportable by reason of having committed any offense covered in section 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii), (A)(iii), (B), (C), or (D) of this title,</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(C) is deportable under section 1227(a)(2)(A)(i) of this title on the basis of an offense for which the alien has been sentence[d] to a term of imprisonment of at least 1 year, or</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(D) is inadmissible under section 1182(a)(3)(B) of this title or deportable under section 1227(a)(4)(B) of this title,</p>
<p>"when the alien is released, without regard to whether the alien is released on parole, supervised release, or probation, and without regard to whether the alien may be arrested or imprisoned again for the same offense.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(2) Release</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The Attorney General <em>may release </em>an alien described in paragraph (1) <em>only if </em>the Attorney General decides <em>pursuant to section 3521</em> of title 18 that release of the alien from custody is necessary to provide protection to a witness, a potential witness, a person cooperating with an investigation into major criminal activity, or an immediate family member or close associate of a witness, potential witness, or person cooperating with such an investigation, and the alien satisfies the Attorney General that the alien will not pose a danger to the safety of other persons or of property and is likely to appear for any scheduled proceeding.  A decision relating to such release shall take place in accordance with a procedure that considers the severity of the offense committed by the alien."  (Emphasis added.) </p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">3</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER"><em>Statute Applicable to Miscellaneous Applicants for Admission</em></p>
</h3><p>8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1225.  "Inspection by immigration officers; expedited removal of inadmissible arriving aliens; referral for hearing</p>
<p align="CENTER">.....</p>
<p> "(b) Inspection of applicants for admission</p>
<p align="CENTER">.....</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(2) Inspection of other aliens</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(A) In general</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Subject to subparagraphs (B) and (C), in the case of an alien who is an applicant for admission, if the examining immigration officer determines that an alien seeking admission is not clearly and beyond a doubt entitled to be admitted, the alien <em>shall be detained </em>for a proceeding under section 1229a of this title.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(B) Exception</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Subparagraph (A) shall not apply to an alien--</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(i) who is a crewman,</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(ii) to whom paragraph (1) applies, or</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(iii) who is a stowaway.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(C) Treatment of aliens arriving from contiguous territory</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"In the case of an alien described in subparagraph (A) who is arriving on land (whether or not at a designated port of arrival) from a foreign territory contiguous to the United States, the Attorney General may return the alien to that territory pending a proceeding under section 1229a of this title."  (Emphasis added.)</p>

<p>R.&nbsp;I. Const., Art. 1, 9; R.&nbsp;I. Gen. Laws 12-13-1, 12-22-12 (2002); R.&nbsp;I. Super. Ct. Rule Crim. Proc. 46 (Supp. 2017)</p>

<p>South Carolina</p>

<p>S.&nbsp;C. Const., Art. 1, 15; S.&nbsp;C. Code Ann. 17-15-10, 22-5-510 (Cum. Supp. 2017), 18-1-90 (2014)</p>

<p>South Dakota</p>

<p>S.&nbsp;D. Const., Art. 6, 8; S.&nbsp;D. Codified Laws 23A-43-2, 23A-43-2.1, 23A-43-16 (2016), 23A-43-3, 23A-43-4 (Cum. Supp. 2017)</p>

<p>Utah Const., Art. 1, 8; Utah Code 77-20-1, 77-20-10 (2017)</p>

<p>Va. Code Ann. 19.2-120, 19.2-120.1, 19.2-121, 19.2-319 (2015)</p>

<p></p><hr>


<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>*&nbsp;J<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>ustice Sotomayor </small></span>joins only Part III-C of this opinion.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Anyone who believes that he is not covered by 1226(c) may also ask for what is known as a "<em>Joseph</em> hearing."  See <em>Matter of Joseph</em>, 22 I.&nbsp;&amp;&nbsp;N. Dec. 799 (BIA 1999).  At a <em>Joseph</em> hearing, that person "may avoid mandatory detention by demonstrating that he is not an alien, was not convicted of the predicate crime, or that the [Government] is otherwise substantially unlikely to establish that he is in fact subject to mandatory detention."  <em>Demore</em> v. <em>Kim</em>, 538 U.&nbsp;S. 510, 514, n.&nbsp;3 (2003).  Whether respondents are entitled to <em>Joseph</em> hearings is not before this Court.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It is questionable whether this is true for aliens who are detained under 8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1225(b)(1)(B)(ii) for consideration of their asylum applications.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The concurrence contends that "detention <em>is</em> an 'action taken .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. to remove' an alien" and that therefore "even the narrowest reading of 'arising from' must cover" the claims raised by respondents.  <em>Post,</em> at 6.  We do not follow this logic.  We will assume for the sake of argument that detention is an action taken "to remove an alien," <em>i.e.</em>, for the purpose of removing an alien, rather than simply an action aimed at ensuring that the alien does not flee or commit a crime while his proceedings are pending.  But even if we proceed on the basis of that assumption, we do not see what it proves.  The question is not whether <em>detention</em> is an action taken to remove an alien but whether <em>the legal questions</em> in this case arise from such an action.  And for the reasons explained above, those legal questions are too remote from the actions taken to fall within the scope of 1252(b)(9).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;According to the dissent, we could have applied the <em>expressio unius</em> canon in <em>Zadvydas</em> as well because there was also an "alternative avenue for relief, namely, bail," available for aliens detained under 1231(a)(6).  <em>Post, </em>at 25 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer, J.</small></span>).  But the dissent overlooks the fact that the provision granting bail was precisely the same provision that the Court purported to be interpreting, so the canon was not applicable.  See 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 683.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Uniting and Strengthening America by Providing Appropriate Tools Required to Intercept and Obstruct Terrorism Act of 2001 (PATRIOT Act), 115 Stat. 272.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As the dissent notes, 1158(d)(2) regulates employment authorization for certain "applicant[s] for asylum."  Were all asylum applicants detained, the dissent says, that provision would make no sense, because detained aliens do not need work authorizations.  <em>Post,</em> at 23-24.  But 1158(d)(2) applies not only to aliens seeking asylum status "in accordance with .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. section 1225(b)" (and thus aliens who are detained), but also to all aliens already "physically present in the United States."  1158(a)(1).  Many of those aliens will be in the country lawfully, and thus they will not be detained and will be able to work pending the outcome of their asylum application.  For example, an alien may apply for asylum after being admitted into the country on a short-term visa.  While the application is pending, 1158 may offer a way for that alien to find employment.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In response, the dissent accuses us of "apply[ing] this provision to some asylum applicants but not the ones before us."  <em>Post, </em>at 23-24.  That is not remotely what we are doing.  We do not doubt that 1158(d)(2) "applies" to all "applicant[s] for asylum" as it says, even if some of those applicants are not as likely to receive an employment authorization (for instance, because they are detained) as others.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It should not be surprising by this point that even the aliens in <em>Waldman</em> understood "detention" in contradistinction to "bail."  See Petition for Rehearing in <em>Tod</em> v. <em>Waldman</em>, O.&nbsp;T. 1924, No. 95, pp.&nbsp;17-18 ("[T]he Court's mandate should authorize relators to give bail, instead of having [them] go to Ellis Island and remain there <em>in custody</em> pending an appeal .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. which may involve very long <em>detention</em> pending hearing of the appeal .&nbsp;.&nbsp;." (capitalization omitted and emphasis added)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent argues that because "the question at issue [in <em>Zadvydas</em>] was release from detention," "the key word was consequently 'may.'&nbsp;"  <em>Post, </em>at 23.  We agree but fail to see the point.  If, as the dissent admits, <em>Zadvydas</em> was about "release from detention" and not about what qualifies as "detention," then it is unclear why the dissent thinks that decision supports its unorthodox interpretation of the word "detention."</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The named Government officials are the Attorney General of the United States, the Secretary of the Department of Homeland Security, the Director of the Executive Office for Immigration Review, the Director and Assistant Director of the Los Angeles District of Immigration and Customs Enforcement, and several directors of jails and detention facilities.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Section 1252 provides a few specific grants of jurisdiction beyond 1252(a)(1)'s general grant of jurisdiction over final removal orders and all other related questions of law and fact.  Section 1252(b)(7), for example, allows an alien to challenge the validity of his removal order during criminal proceedings if he is charged with willfully failing to depart the United States.  And 1252(e)(2) allows an alien who is denied admission to the United States and ordered removed to raise certain claims in habeas corpus proceedings.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Respondents also asserted at oral argument that the Government "has said repeatedly" that 1252(b)(9) does not apply to detention claims.  Tr. of Oral Arg. 36.  But our "independent obligation" to evaluate jurisdiction, <em>Arbaugh </em>v. <em>Y &amp; H Corp.</em>, 546 U.&nbsp;S. 500, 514 (2006), means that we cannot accept the Government's concessions on this point.  See <em>King Bridge Co.</em> v. <em>Otoe County</em>, 120 U.&nbsp;S. 225, 226 (1887).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Contrary to respondents' argument, some of the respondents will get review before "all the detention has already happened."  Respondents who successfully petition for review to the Court of Appeals from a final removal order and obtain a remand to the immigration court, like class representative Alejandro Rodriguez did here, will have an opportunity to obtain review of their detention before it is complete.  See Third Amended Complaint, at 9-12.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g., </em>Act of Aug. 18, 1884, 28 Stat. 390 ("In every case where an alien is excluded from admission into the United States under any law or treaty now existing or hereinafter made, the decision of the appropriate immigration or customs officers, if adverse to the admission of such alien, shall be final, unless reversed on appeal to the Secretary of Treasury"), upheld in <em>Lem Moon Sing</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 158 U.&nbsp;S. 538, 547-550 (1895); Immigration Act of 1891, 8, 26 Stat. 1085 ("All decisions made by the inspection officers or their assistants touching the right of any alien to land, when adverse to such right, shall be final unless appeal be taken to the superintendent of immigration, whose action shall be subject to review by the Secretary of Treasury"), upheld in <em>Ekiu</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 142 U.&nbsp;S. 651, 660 (1892); 1917 Immigration Act, 19, 39 Stat. 890 ("In every case where any person is ordered deported from the United States under the provisions of this Act, or of any law or treaty, the decision of the Secretary of Labor shall be final"), upheld in <em>Heikkila</em>, 345 U.&nbsp;S., at 233-235, 237.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;I take no position on whether some of the respondents will face other jurisdictional hurdles, even on review of their final removal orders.  See, <em>e.g., </em>1252(a)(2)(A), (B).  I also continue to agree with Justice O'Connor's concurring opinion in <em>Demore </em>v.<em> Kim</em>, 538 U.&nbsp;S. 510 (2003), which explained that 1226(e) "unequivocally deprives federal courts of jurisdiction to set aside 'any action or decision' by the Attorney General" regarding detention.  <em>Id.</em>, at 533 (opinion concurring in part and concurring in judgment).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This Court has never addressed whether habeas relief can be pursued in a class action.  See <em>Schall</em> v. <em>Martin</em>, 467 U.&nbsp;S. 253, 261, n.&nbsp;10 (1984) (reserving this question).  I take no position on that issue here, since I conclude that respondents are not seeking habeas relief in the first place.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

    <div class="case-information">
        <h2 class="case-information__title"> JENNINGS <span style="TEXT-TRANSFORM: UPPERCASE;"><small>ET AL</small></span>.<em> v. RODRIGUEZ </em><span style="TEXT-TRANSFORM: UPPERCASE;"><small>ET AL., INDIVIDUALLY AND ON BEHALF OF ALL OTHERS SIMILARLY SITUATED</small></span> (2018)</h2>
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Docket No:</b> No. 15-1204</p>
        
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Argued:</b> November 30, 2016</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Decided:</b> February 27, 2018</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Court:</b> United States Supreme Court</p>
    </div>

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search

        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);

          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');

            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {

              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {

          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";

          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);

      });

      });
